<th colspan="2" class="summary" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:130%;">Armenian Genocide</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Part of the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Armenianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Armenianism">persecution of Armenians</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcharmenians.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Marcharmenians.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/300px-Marcharmenians.jpg" width="300" height="189" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/450px-Marcharmenians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Marcharmenians.jpg/600px-Marcharmenians.jpg 2x" data-file-width="665" data-file-height="420" /></a>
<div>Armenian civilians, escorted by Ottoman soldiers, marched through Harput (Kharpert) to a prison in nearby Mezireh (present-day <a href="/wiki/El%C3%A2z%C4%B1%C4%9F" class="mw-redirect" title="Elâzığ">Elâzığ</a>), April 1915.</div>
</td>
</tr>

<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Genocide" title="Template talk:Genocide"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Genocide&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

</td>

<p>The <b>Armenian Genocide</b><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>: <span lang="hy">Հայոց ցեղասպանություն</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[note 3]</a></sup> <i>Hayots tseghaspanutyun</i>), also known as the <b>Armenian Holocaust</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup> was the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a>'s systematic extermination of 1.5 million <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-fatalities_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fatalities-12">[note 2]</a></sup> mostly Ottoman citizens within the Ottoman Empire and its successor state, the <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost.com_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost.com-17">[14]</a></sup> The starting date is conventionally held to be 24 April 1915, the day that Ottoman authorities rounded up, arrested, and deported <a href="/wiki/Deportation_of_Armenian_intellectuals_on_24_April_1915" title="Deportation of Armenian intellectuals on 24 April 1915">235 to 270 Armenian intellectuals and community leaders</a> from <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a> to the region of <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>, the majority of whom were eventually murdered. The genocide was carried out during and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and implemented in two phases: the wholesale killing of the able-bodied male population through massacre and subjection of army conscripts to forced labour, followed by the deportation of women, children, the elderly, and the infirm on <a href="/wiki/Death_march" title="Death march">death marches</a> leading to the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian desert">Syrian desert</a>. Driven forward by military escorts, the deportees were deprived of food and water and subjected to periodic robbery, rape, and massacre.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> and Christian ethnic groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Ottoman Greeks</a> were similarly targeted for extermination by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">Assyrian genocide</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a>, and their treatment is considered by some historians to be part of the same genocidal policy.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jones2010_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2010-22">[19]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Armenian_diaspora" title="Armenian diaspora">Armenian diaspora</a> communities around the world came into being as a direct result of the genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Raphael_Lemkin" title="Raphael Lemkin">Raphael Lemkin</a> was explicitly moved by the Armenian annihilation to define systematic and premeditated exterminations within legal parameters and to coin the word <i><a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a></i> in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> The Armenian Genocide is acknowledged to have been one of the first modern genocides,<sup id="cite_ref-24.04.1998_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24.04.1998-26">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-27">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IAGS_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAGS-28">[25]</a></sup> because scholars point to the organized manner in which the killings were carried out in order to eliminate the Armenians, and it is the second most-studied case of genocide after the <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nazi_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nazi-29">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Successor_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Successor state">successor state</a> of the Ottoman Empire, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide_denial" title="Armenian Genocide denial">denies</a> the word <i>genocide</i> as an accurate term for the mass killings of Armenians that began under Ottoman rule in 1915. It has in recent years been faced with repeated calls to recognize them as genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> To date, 29 countries have <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide_recognition" title="Armenian Genocide recognition">officially recognized the mass killings as genocide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> as have most genocide scholars and historians.<sup id="cite_ref-Monroe2012_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monroe2012-32">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Frey2009_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frey2009-34">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Armenians in the Ottoman Empire">Armenians in the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Armenian_population" title="Ottoman Armenian population">Ottoman Armenian population</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenians_under_Ottoman_rule">Armenians under Ottoman rule</span></h3>
<p>The western portion of historical Armenia, known as <a href="/wiki/Western_Armenia" title="Western Armenia">Western Armenia</a>, had come under Ottoman rule following the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Peace of Amasya</a> (1555) and decisively with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a> (1639).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Henceforth, the region was also alternatively referred to as Turkish Armenia or Ottoman Armenia.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> The vast majority of Armenians, grouped together under the name Armenian <i><a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a></i> (community) and led by their spiritual head, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Armenian Patriarch of Constantinople">Armenian Patriarch of Constantinople</a>, were mainly concentrated in the eastern provinces of the Ottoman Empire, although large communities were also found in the western provinces, as well as in the capital <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a>.</p>
<p>The Armenian community was made up of three religious denominations: <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic_Church" title="Armenian Catholic Church">Armenian Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Evangelical_Church" title="Armenian Evangelical Church">Armenian Protestant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic</a>, the religion of a vast majority of Armenians. Through the millet system, the Armenian community were allowed to rule themselves under their own system of governance with fairly little interference from the Ottoman government. With the exception of the empire's urban centers and the extremely wealthy, Constantinople-based <i>Amira</i> class, a social elite whose members included the Duzians (Directors of the Imperial Mint), the <a href="/wiki/Balyan_family" title="Balyan family">Balyans</a> (Chief Imperial Architects) and the <a href="/wiki/Dadian_family" title="Dadian family">Dadians</a> (Superintendent of the Gunpowder Mills and manager of industrial factories), most Armenians – approximately 70% of their population – lived in poor and dangerous conditions in the rural countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barsoumian_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barsoumian-39">[36]</a></sup> Ottoman census figures clash with the statistics collected by the Armenian Patriarchate. According to the latter, there were almost three million Armenians living in the empire in 1878 (400,000 in Constantinople and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, 600,000 in Asia Minor and <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, 670,000 in <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Armenia" title="Lesser Armenia">Lesser Armenia</a> and the area near <a href="/wiki/Kayseri" title="Kayseri">Kayseri</a>, and 1,300,000 in Western Armenia itself).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the eastern provinces, the Armenians were subject to the whims of their Turkish and <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> neighbors, who would regularly overtax them, subject them to <a href="/wiki/Brigandage" title="Brigandage">brigandage</a> and kidnapping, force them to convert to Islam, and otherwise exploit them without interference from central or local authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Barsoumian_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barsoumian-39">[36]</a></sup> In the Ottoman Empire, in accordance with the <i><a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">dhimmi</a></i> system implemented in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslim</a> countries, they, like all other Christians and also Jews, were accorded certain freedoms. The dhimmi system in the Ottoman Empire was largely based upon the <a href="/wiki/Pact_of_Umar" title="Pact of Umar">Pact of Umar</a>. The client status established the rights of the non-Muslims to property, livelihood and freedom of worship but they were in essence treated as <a href="/wiki/Second-class_citizen" title="Second-class citizen">second-class citizens</a> in the empire and referred to in Turkish as <i><a href="/wiki/Giaour" title="Giaour">gavours</a></i>, a pejorative word meaning "<a href="/wiki/Infidel" title="Infidel">infidel</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Unbeliever" class="mw-redirect" title="Unbeliever">unbeliever</a>". While the Pact of Umar prohibited non-Muslims from building new places of worship, it was not enforced in all regions of the Ottoman Empire. Since there were no laws concerning religious ghettos, the prohibition of non-Muslims building new places of worship led to their clustering around existing ones.<sup id="cite_ref-.CC.81gostonMasters2010_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.CC.81gostonMasters2010-41">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup> Writing in the late 1890s after a visit to the Ottoman Empire, the British ethnographer <a href="/wiki/William_Mitchell_Ramsay" title="William Mitchell Ramsay">William Ramsay</a> described the conditions of Armenian life as follows:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We must, however, go back to an older time, if we want to appreciate what uncontrolled Turkish rule meant, alike to Armenians and to Greeks. It did not mean religious persecution; it meant unutterable contempt&#160;... They were dogs and pigs; and their nature was to be Christians, to be spat upon, if their shadow darkened a Turk, to be outraged, to be the mats on which he wiped the mud from his feet. Conceive the inevitable result of centuries of slavery, of subjection to insult and scorn, centuries in which nothing that belonged to the Armenian, neither his property, his house, his life, his person, nor his family, was sacred or safe from violence – capricious, unprovoked violence – to resist which by violence meant death!<sup id="cite_ref-Ramsay_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsay-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In addition to other legal limitations, Christians were not considered equals to Muslims and several prohibitions were placed on them. Their testimony against Muslims by Christians and Jews was inadmissible in courts of law wherein a Muslim could be punished; this meant that their testimony could only be considered in commercial cases. They were forbidden to carry weapons or ride atop horses and camels. Their houses could not overlook those of Muslims; and their religious practices were severely circumscribed (e.g., the ringing of church bells was strictly forbidden).<sup id="cite_ref-.CC.81gostonMasters2010_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.CC.81gostonMasters2010-41">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_implementation.2C_1840s.E2.80.9380s">Reform implementation, 1840s–80s</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Armenian_Genocide&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(April 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Question" title="Armenian Question">Armenian Question</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg/220px-Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg/330px-Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg/440px-Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1270" data-file-height="795" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethnic_map_of_Asia_Minor_and_Caucasus_in_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German ethnographic map of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> in 1914. Armenians are labeled in blue.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the mid-19th century, the three major European powers, Great Britain, France and Russia, began to question the Empire's treatment of its Christian minorities and pressure it to grant equal rights to all its subjects. From 1839 to the declaration of a constitution in 1876, the Ottoman government instituted the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a>, a series of reforms designed to improve the status of minorities. Nevertheless, most of the reforms were never implemented because the empire's Muslim population rejected the principle of equality for Christians. By the late 1870s, the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, along with several other Christian nations in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, frustrated with their conditions, had, often with the help of the <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">great powers</a>, broken free of Ottoman rule. The Armenians remained, by and large, passive during these years, earning them the title of <i>millet-i sadika</i> or the "loyal millet".<sup id="cite_ref-Dadrian_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dadrian-45">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1860s and early 1870s this passivity gave way to new currents of thinking in Armenian society. Led by intellectuals educated at European universities or American missionary schools in Turkey, Armenians began to question their second-class status and press for better treatment from their government. In one such instance, after amassing the signatures of peasants from Western Armenia, the Armenian Communal Council petitioned the Ottoman government to redress their principal grievances: "the looting and murder in Armenian towns by [Muslim] <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>, improprieties during tax collection, criminal behavior by government officials and the refusal to accept Christians as witnesses in trial". The Ottoman government considered these grievances and promised to punish those responsible, though no meaningful steps were ever taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36</sup></p>
<p>Following the violent suppression of Christians in the uprisings in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> in 1875, the Great Powers invoked the 1856 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1856)" title="Treaty of Paris (1856)">Treaty of Paris</a> by claiming that it gave them the right to intervene and protect the Ottoman Empire's Christian minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35ff</sup> Under growing pressure, the government of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Sultan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a> declared itself a constitutional monarchy with a parliament (which was almost immediately <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#Common_procedure" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a>) and entered into negotiations with the powers. At the same time, the Armenian patriarch of Constantinople, Nerses II, forwarded Armenian complaints of widespread "forced land seizure&#160;... forced conversion of women and children, arson, <a href="/wiki/Protection_racket" title="Protection racket">protection extortion</a>, rape, and murder" to the Powers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:37</sup></p>
<p>The Russo-Turkish War of 1877–78 ended with Russia's decisive victory and its army in occupation of large parts of eastern Turkey, but not before entire Armenian districts had been devastated by massacres carried out with the connivance of Ottoman authorities. In the wake of these events, Patriarch Nerses and his emissaries made repeated approaches to Russian leaders to urge the inclusion of a clause granting local self-government to the Armenians in the forthcoming <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Stefano" title="Treaty of San Stefano">Treaty of San Stefano</a>. The Russians were receptive and drew up the clause, but the Ottomans flatly rejected it during negotiations. In its place, the two sides agreed on a clause making the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Sublime Porte</a>'s implementation of reforms in the Armenian provinces a condition of Russia's withdrawal, thus designating Russia the guarantor of the reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> The clause entered the treaty as Article 16 and marked the first appearance of what came to be known in European diplomacy as the Armenian question.</p>
<p>On receiving a copy of the treaty, Britain promptly objected to it and particularly Article 16, which it saw as ceding too much influence to Russia. It immediately pushed for a congress of the great powers to be convened to discuss and revise the treaty, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> in June–July 1878. Patriarch Nerses sent a delegation led by his distinguished predecessor, Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Khrimian_Hayrik" class="mw-redirect" title="Khrimian Hayrik">Khrimian Hayrik</a>, to speak for the Armenians, but it was not admitted into the sessions on the grounds that it did not represent a country. Confined to the periphery, the delegation did its best to contact the representatives of the powers and argue the case for Armenian administrative autonomy within the Ottoman Empire, but to little effect.</p>
<p>Following an understanding reached with Ottoman representatives, Britain drew up an emasculated version of Article 16 to replace the original, a clause that retained the call for reforms but omitted any reference to the Russian occupation, thereby dispensing with the principal guarantee of their implementation. Despite an ambiguous reference to great power supervision, the clause failed to offset the removal of the Russian guarantee with any tangible equivalent, thus leaving the timing and fate of the reforms to the discretion of the Sublime Porte.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–39</sup> The clause was readily adopted as Article 61 of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a> on the last day of the Congress, to the deep disappointment of the Armenian delegation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenian_national_liberation_movement">Armenian national liberation movement</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Armenian_national_liberation_movement" title="Armenian national liberation movement">Armenian national liberation movement</a></div>
<p>Prospects for reforms faded rapidly following the signing of the Berlin treaty, as security conditions in the Armenian provinces went from bad to worse and abuses proliferated. Upset with this turn of events, a number of disillusioned Armenian intellectuals living in Europe and Russia decided to form political parties and societies dedicated to the betterment of their compatriots in the Ottoman Empire. In the last quarter of the 19th century, this movement came to be dominated by three parties: the <a href="/wiki/Armenakan_Party" title="Armenakan Party">Armenakan</a>, whose influence was limited to Van, the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democrat_Hunchakian_Party" title="Social Democrat Hunchakian Party">Social Democrat Hunchakian Party</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Revolutionary_Federation" title="Armenian Revolutionary Federation">Armenian Revolutionary Federation</a> (Dashnaktsutiun). Ideological differences aside, all the parties had the common goal of achieving better social conditions for the Armenians of the Ottoman Empire through self-defense<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> and advocating increased European pressure on the Ottoman government to implement the promised reforms.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hamidian_massacres.2C_1894.E2.80.9396">Hamidian massacres, 1894–96</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">Hamidian massacres</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1895erzurum-victims.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/1895erzurum-victims.jpg/220px-1895erzurum-victims.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/1895erzurum-victims.jpg/330px-1895erzurum-victims.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/1895erzurum-victims.jpg/440px-1895erzurum-victims.jpg 2x" data-file-width="635" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1895erzurum-victims.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Corpses of massacred Armenians in <a href="/wiki/Erzurum" title="Erzurum">Erzurum</a> in 1895</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1876, the Ottoman state had been led by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a>. Immediately after the Treaty of Berlin was signed, Abdul Hamid attempted to forestall implementation of its reform provisions by asserting that Armenians did not make up a majority in the provinces and that their reports of abuses were largely exaggerated or false. In 1890, Abdul Hamid created a paramilitary outfit known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Hamidiye_(cavalry)" title="Hamidiye (cavalry)">Hamidiye</a></i> which was mostly made up of Kurdish irregulars who were tasked to "deal with the Armenians as they wished".<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:40</sup> As Ottoman officials intentionally provoked rebellions (often as a result of over-taxation) in Armenian populated towns, such as in <a href="/wiki/1894_Sasun_rebellion" title="1894 Sasun rebellion">Sasun</a> in 1894 and <a href="/wiki/1894_Sasun_rebellion" title="1894 Sasun rebellion">Zeitun</a> in 1895–96, these regiments were increasingly used to deal with the Armenians by way of oppression and massacre. In some instances Armenians successfully fought off the regiments and in 1895 brought the excesses to the attention of the Great Powers, who subsequently condemned the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Porte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:40–42</sup></p>
<p>In October 1895, the Powers forced Abdul Hamid to sign a new reform package designed to curtail the powers of the Hamidiye but, like the Berlin Treaty, it was never implemented. On 1 October 1895, 2,000 Armenians assembled in Constantinople to petition for the implementation of the reforms, but Ottoman police units converged on the rally and violently broke it up.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:57–58</sup> Soon, massacres of Armenians broke out in Constantinople and then engulfed the rest of the Armenian-populated provinces of <a href="/wiki/Bitlis" title="Bitlis">Bitlis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diyarbekir" class="mw-redirect" title="Diyarbekir">Diyarbekir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzurum" title="Erzurum">Erzurum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harput" class="mw-redirect" title="Harput">Harput</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sivas" title="Sivas">Sivas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trabzon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Van,_Turkey" title="Van, Turkey">Van</a>. Estimates differ on how many Armenians were killed but European documentation of the <a href="/wiki/Pogroms" class="mw-redirect" title="Pogroms">pogroms</a>, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">Hamidian massacres</a>, placed the figures at between 100,000 and 300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Hamid was never directly implicated in ordering the massacres, it is believed that they had his tacit approval.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:42</sup> Frustrated with European indifference to the massacres, a group of members of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Revolutionary_Federation" title="Armenian Revolutionary Federation">Armenian Revolutionary Federation</a> <a href="/wiki/1896_Ottoman_Bank_Takeover" class="mw-redirect" title="1896 Ottoman Bank Takeover">seized</a> the European-managed <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Bank" title="Ottoman Bank">Ottoman Bank</a> on 26 August 1896. This incident brought further sympathy for Armenians in Europe and was lauded by the European and American press, which vilified Hamid and painted him as the "great assassin", "bloody Sultan", and "<a href="/wiki/Abdul_the_Damned" title="Abdul the Damned">Abdul the Damned</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35, 115</sup> While the Great Powers vowed to take action and enforce new reforms, these never came to fruition due to conflicting political and economic interests.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude_to_the_Genocide">Prelude to the Genocide</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Young_Turk_Revolution_of_1908">The Young Turk Revolution of 1908</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg/220px-Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg/330px-Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg/440px-Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="347" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Armenians_1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Armenians of <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a> celebrating the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Union_and_Progress" title="Committee of Union and Progress">CUP</a> government.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 24 July 1908, Armenians' hopes for equality in the empire brightened once more when a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> staged by officers in the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Third_Army_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Third Army (Ottoman Empire)">Third Army</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Salonika" class="mw-redirect" title="Salonika">Salonika</a> removed Abdul Hamid II from power and restored the country to a constitutional monarchy. The officers were part of the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turks" title="Young Turks">Young Turk</a> movement that wanted to reform administration of the perceived <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">decadent</a> state of the Ottoman Empire and modernize it to European standards.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> The movement was an anti-Hamidian coalition made up of two distinct groups, the liberal <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">constitutionalists</a> and the nationalists. The former were more democratic and accepting of Armenians, whereas the latter were less tolerant of Armenians and their frequent requests for European assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:140–41</sup> In 1902, during a congress of the Young Turks held in Paris, the heads of the liberal wing, <a href="/wiki/Prens_Sabahaddin" class="mw-redirect" title="Prens Sabahaddin">Sabahaddin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Riza" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Riza">Ahmed Riza</a> Bey, partially persuaded the nationalists to include in their objectives ensuring some rights for all the minorities of the empire.</p>
<p>One of the numerous factions within the Young Turk movement was a secret revolutionary organization called the <i><a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Union_and_Progress" title="Committee of Union and Progress">Committee of Union and Progress</a></i> (CUP). It drew its membership from disaffected army officers based in <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Salonika</a> and was behind a wave of mutinies against the central government. In 1908, elements of the Third Army and the Second Army Corps declared their opposition to the Sultan and threatened to march on the capital to depose him. Hamid, shaken by the wave of resentment, stepped down from power as <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> alike rejoiced in his dethronement.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:143–44</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Adana_massacre_of_1909">The Adana massacre of 1909</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adana_massacre" title="Adana massacre">Adana massacre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg/220px-AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg/330px-AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg/440px-AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5223" data-file-height="3176" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AdanaChristianQuarter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Armenian quarter after the massacres in <a href="/wiki/Adana" title="Adana">Adana</a> in 1909.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_countercoup_of_1909" title="Ottoman countercoup of 1909">countercoup took place</a> in early 1909, ultimately resulting in the <a href="/wiki/31_March_Incident" title="31 March Incident">31 March Incident</a> on 13 April 1909. Some reactionary Ottoman military elements, joined by <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Theological" class="mw-redirect" title="Theological">theological</a> students, aimed to return control of the country to the Sultan and the rule of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a>. Riots and fighting broke out between the reactionary forces and CUP forces, until the CUP was able to put down the uprising and <a href="/wiki/Court-martial" title="Court-martial">court-martial</a> the opposition leaders.</p>
<p>While the movement initially targeted the Young Turk government, it spilled over into pogroms against Armenians who were perceived as having supported the restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:68–69</sup> When Ottoman Army troops were called in, many accounts record that instead of trying to quell the violence they actually took part in pillaging Armenian enclaves in <a href="/wiki/Adana" title="Adana">Adana</a> province.<sup id="cite_ref-daysof_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daysof-52">[49]</a></sup> The number of Armenians killed in the course of the <a href="/wiki/Adana_massacre" title="Adana massacre">Adana massacre</a> ranged between 15,000 and 30,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:69</sup><sup id="cite_ref-30t_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30t-53">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Balkan_Wars">The Balkan Wars</span></h3>
<p>In 1912, the <a href="/wiki/First_Balkan_War" title="First Balkan War">First Balkan War</a> broke out and ended with the defeat of the Ottoman Empire as well as the loss of 85% of its European territory. Many in the empire saw their defeat as "Allah's divine punishment for a society that did not know how to pull itself together".<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:84</sup> The Turkish nationalist movement in the country gradually came to view <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> as their last refuge. That the Armenian population formed a significant minority in this region later figured prominently in the calculations of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Pashas" title="Three Pashas">Three Pashas</a>, who carried out the Armenian Genocide.</p>
<p>An important consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a> was also the mass expulsion of Muslims (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Muhacir" title="Muhacir">muhacirs</a></i>) from the Balkans. Beginning in the mid-19th century, hundreds of thousands of Muslims, including <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chechens" title="Chechens">Chechens</a>, were expelled or forced to flee from the Caucasus and the Balkans (<a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a>) as a result of the Russo-Turkish wars and the conflicts in the Balkans. Muslim society in the empire was incensed by this flood of refugees. A journal published in Constantinople expressed the mood of the times: "Let this be a warning&#160;... O Muslims, don't get comfortable! Do not let your blood cool before taking revenge".<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:86</sup> As many as 850,000 of these refugees were settled in areas where the Armenians were resident from the period of 1878–1904. The <i>muhacirs</i> resented the status of their relatively well-off neighbors and, as historian <a href="/wiki/Taner_Ak%C3%A7am" title="Taner Akçam">Taner Akçam</a> and others have noted, the refugees came to play a pivotal role in the killings of the Armenians and the <a href="/wiki/Confiscated_Armenian_properties_in_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Confiscated Armenian properties in Turkey">confiscation of their properties</a> during the genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:86–87</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="Middle Eastern theatre of World War I">Middle Eastern theatre of World War I</a></div>
<p>On 2 November 1914, the Ottoman Empire opened the Middle Eastern theater of World War I by entering hostilities on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> and against the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>. The battles of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Campaign" title="Caucasus Campaign">Caucasus Campaign</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Campaign" title="Persian Campaign">Persian Campaign</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a> affected several populous Armenian centers. Before entering the war, the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> had sent representatives to the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_congress_at_Erzurum" title="Armenian congress at Erzurum">Armenian congress at Erzurum</a> to persuade Ottoman Armenians to facilitate its conquest of <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a> by inciting an insurrection of Russian Armenians against the Russian army in the event a <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Campaign" title="Caucasus Campaign">Caucasus front</a> was opened.<sup id="cite_ref-Ak.C3.A7am_44-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ak.C3.A7am-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:136</sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Sarikamish">Battle of Sarikamish</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarikamish" title="Battle of Sarikamish">Battle of Sarikamish</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Campaign" title="Caucasus Campaign">Caucasus Campaign</a></div>
<p>On 24 December 1914, Minister of War <a href="/wiki/Enver_Pasha" title="Enver Pasha">Enver Pasha</a> implemented a plan to encircle and destroy the Russian <a href="/wiki/Russian_Caucasus_Army_(World_War_I)" title="Russian Caucasus Army (World War I)">Caucasus Army</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sarikamish" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarikamish">Sarikamish</a> in order to regain territories lost to Russia after the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%931878)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877–1878)">Russo-Turkish War</a> of 1877–78. Enver Pasha's forces were routed in the battle, and almost completely destroyed. Returning to Constantinople, Enver Pasha publicly blamed his defeat on Armenians in the region having actively sided with the Russians.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:200</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Directive_8682.2C_25_February">Directive 8682, 25 February</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_labour_battalions" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman labour battalions">Ottoman labour battalions</a></div>
<p>On 25 February 1915, the Ottoman General Staff released the War Minister Enver Pasha's <i>Directive 8682</i> on "Increased security and precautions" to all military units calling for the removal of all ethnic Armenians serving in the Ottoman forces from their posts and for their demobilization. They were assigned to the unarmed <a href="/wiki/Labour_battalion" title="Labour battalion">Labour battalions</a> (Turkish: <i>amele taburları</i>). The directive accused the Armenian Patriarchate of releasing State secrets to the Russians. Enver Pasha explained this decision as "out of fear that they would collaborate with the Russians".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> Traditionally, the Ottoman Army only drafted non-Muslim males between the ages of 20 and 45 into the regular army. The younger (15–20) and older (45–60) non-Muslim soldiers had always been used as logistical support through the labour battalions. Before February, some of the Armenian recruits were utilized as labourers (<i>hamals</i>), though they would ultimately be executed.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Transferring Armenian conscripts from active combat to passive, unarmed logistic sections was an important precursor to the subsequent genocide. As reported in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Memoirs_of_Naim_Bey" title="The Memoirs of Naim Bey">The Memoirs of Naim Bey</a></i>, the execution of the Armenians in these battalions was part of a premeditated strategy of the CUP. Many of these Armenian recruits were executed by local Turkish gangs.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:178</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Van.2C_April_1915">Van, April 1915</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Defense_of_Van_(1915)" title="Defense of Van (1915)">Siege of Van</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Van_Defenders.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Van_Defenders.jpg/220px-Van_Defenders.jpg" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Van_Defenders.jpg/330px-Van_Defenders.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Van_Defenders.jpg/440px-Van_Defenders.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="464" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Van_Defenders.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Armed Armenian civilians and self-defense units during the <a href="/wiki/Defense_of_Van_(1915)" title="Defense of Van (1915)">Siege of Van</a> in April–May 1915</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 19 April 1915, <a href="/wiki/Jevdet_Bey" class="mw-redirect" title="Jevdet Bey">Jevdet Bey</a> demanded that the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Van" class="mw-redirect" title="City of Van">city of Van</a> immediately furnish him 4,000&#160;soldiers under the pretext of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a>. However, it was clear to the Armenian population that his goal was to massacre the able-bodied men of Van so that there would be no defenders. Jevdet Bey had already used his official writ in nearby villages, ostensibly to search for arms, but in fact to initiate wholesale massacres.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:202</sup> The Armenians offered five hundred soldiers and exemption money for the rest in order to buy time, but Jevdet Bey accused the Armenians of "rebellion" and asserted his determination to "crush" it at any cost. "If the rebels fire a single shot", he declared, "I shall kill every Christian man, woman, and" (pointing to his knee) "every child, up to here".<sup id="cite_ref-Morgenthau_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgenthau-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:205</sup></p>
<p>The next day, 20 April 1915, the siege of Van began when an Armenian woman was harassed, and the two Armenian men who came to her aid were killed by Ottoman soldiers. The Armenian defenders protected the 30,000&#160;residents and 15,000&#160;refugees living in an area of roughly one square kilometer of the Armenian Quarter and suburb of Aigestan with 1,500&#160;ablebodied riflemen who were supplied with 300&#160;rifles and 1,000&#160;pistols and antique weapons. The conflict lasted until <a href="/wiki/General_Yudenich" class="mw-redirect" title="General Yudenich">General Yudenich</a> of Russia came to their rescue.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reports of the conflict reached then <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Turkey" title="United States Ambassador to Turkey">United States Ambassador to the Ottoman Empire</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morgenthau,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Morgenthau, Sr.">Henry Morgenthau, Sr.</a> from <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> and Van, prompting him to raise the issue in person with Talaat and Enver. As he quoted to them the testimonies of his consulate officials, they justified the deportations as necessary to the conduct of the war, suggesting that complicity of the Armenians of Van with the Russian forces that had taken the city justified the persecution of all ethnic Armenians.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_and_deportation_of_Armenian_notables.2C_April_1915">Arrest and deportation of Armenian notables, April 1915</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Deportation_of_Armenian_intellectuals_on_24_April_1915" title="Deportation of Armenian intellectuals on 24 April 1915">Deportation of Armenian intellectuals on 24 April 1915</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:April24Victims.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/April24Victims.jpg/220px-April24Victims.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/April24Victims.jpg/330px-April24Victims.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/April24Victims.jpg/440px-April24Victims.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2186" data-file-height="1154" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:April24Victims.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some Armenian intellectuals arrested on 24 April 1915, and following weeks, then deported and killed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1914, Ottoman authorities had already begun a propaganda drive to present Armenians living in the Ottoman Empire as a threat to the empire's security. An <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Navy" title="Ottoman Navy">Ottoman naval</a> officer in the War Office described the planning:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In order to justify this enormous crime the requisite propaganda material was thoroughly prepared in Istanbul. [It included such statements as] 'the Armenians are in league with the enemy. They will launch an uprising in Istanbul, kill off the Ittihadist leaders and will succeed in opening up the straits [of the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a>]'.<sup id="cite_ref-Dadrian_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dadrian-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:220</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Talat_Pasha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Talat_Pasha.jpg/220px-Talat_Pasha.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Talat_Pasha.jpg/330px-Talat_Pasha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Talat_Pasha.jpg/440px-Talat_Pasha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5316" data-file-height="3808" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Talat_Pasha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior Minister <a href="/wiki/Talaat_Pasha" title="Talaat Pasha">Talaat Pasha</a>, who ordered the arrests.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the night of 23–24 April 1915, known as <a href="/wiki/Red_Sunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Sunday">Red Sunday</a> (<a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>: <span lang="hy">Կարմիր Կիրակի</span> <i>Garmir Giragi</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> rounded up and imprisoned an estimated <a href="/wiki/Deportation_of_Armenian_notables_in_1915" class="mw-redirect" title="Deportation of Armenian notables in 1915">250 Armenian intellectuals and community leaders</a> of the Ottoman capital, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a>, and later those in other centers, who were moved to two holding centers near Ankara.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:211–12</sup> This date coincided with Allied troop landings at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gallipoli" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Gallipoli">Gallipoli</a> after unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Naval_operations_in_the_Dardanelles_Campaign" title="Naval operations in the Dardanelles Campaign">Allied naval attempts to break through the Dardanelles</a> to Constantinople in February and March 1915.</p>
<p>Following the passage of Tehcir Law on 29 May 1915, the Armenian leaders, except for the few who were able to return to Constantinople, were gradually deported and assassinated.<sup id="cite_ref-UngorPolatel2011_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UngorPolatel2011-59">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rae2002_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rae2002-60">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jacobs2009_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs2009-61">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whitehorn2015_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitehorn2015-62">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nelson2005_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2005-63">[60]</a></sup> The date 24 April is commemorated as <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide_Remembrance_Day" title="Armenian Genocide Remembrance Day">Genocide Remembrance Day</a> by Armenians around the world.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deportations">Deportations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tehcir_Law" title="Tehcir Law">Tehcir Law</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Armenian_casualties_of_deportations" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian casualties of deportations">Armenian casualties of deportations</a></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg/800px-Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg.png" width="800" height="393" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg/1200px-Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg/1600px-Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2574" data-file-height="1264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian_Genocide_Map-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of massacre locations and deportation and extermination centers</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In May 1915, <a href="/wiki/Mehmet_Talaat_Pasha" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmet Talaat Pasha">Mehmet Talaat Pasha</a> requested that the cabinet and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Vizier" title="Grand Vizier">Grand Vizier</a> <a href="/wiki/Said_Halim_Pasha" title="Said Halim Pasha">Said Halim Pasha</a> legalize a measure for the deportation of Armenians to other places due to what Talaat Pasha called "the Armenian riots and massacres, which had arisen in a number of places in the country". However, Talaat Pasha was referring specifically to events in <a href="/wiki/Defense_of_Van_(1915)" title="Defense of Van (1915)">Van</a> and extending the implementation to the regions in which alleged "riots and massacres" would affect the security of the war zone of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Campaign" title="Caucasus Campaign">Caucasus Campaign</a>. Later, the scope of the deportation was widened in order to include the Armenians in the other provinces.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg/220px-Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg/330px-Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg/440px-Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="318" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Human_remains_from_the_massacre_of_the_Armenians_at_Erzingan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The remains of Armenians massacred at <a href="/wiki/Erzinjan" class="mw-redirect" title="Erzinjan">Erzinjan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 29 May 1915, the CUP Central Committee passed the <a href="/wiki/Tehcir_Law" title="Tehcir Law">Temporary Law of Deportation</a> ("Tehcir Law"), giving the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> and military authorization to deport anyone it "sensed" as a threat to national security.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:186–88</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg/220px-NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg/330px-NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="353" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NY_Times_Armenian_genocide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Headline of <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a>,</i> December 15, 1915</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the implementation of <a href="/wiki/Tehcir_Law" title="Tehcir Law">Tehcir Law</a>, the confiscation of Armenian property and the slaughter of Armenians that ensued upon its enactment outraged much of the western world. While the Ottoman Empire's wartime allies offered little protest, a wealth of German and Austrian historical documents has since come to attest to the witnesses' horror at the killings and mass starvation of Armenians.<sup id="cite_ref-Fisk_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fisk-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:329–31</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fromkin_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fromkin-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:212–13</sup> In the United States, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported almost daily on the mass murder of the Armenian people, describing the process as "systematic", "authorized" and "organized by the government". <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> would later characterize this as "the greatest crime of the war".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian Hans-Lukas Kieser states that, from the statements of Talaat Pasha<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> it is clear that the officials were aware that the deportation order was genocidal.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup> Another historian <a href="/wiki/Taner_Ak%C3%A7am" title="Taner Akçam">Taner Akçam</a> states that the telegrams show that the overall coordination of the genocide was taken over by Talaat Pasha.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_marches">Death marches</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png/220px-Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png/330px-Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png/440px-Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png 2x" data-file-width="2479" data-file-height="1551" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian_woman_kneeling_beside_dead_child_in_field.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Armenian woman kneeling beside a dead child in field "within sight of help and safety at <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Armenians were marched out to the <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syrian</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Deir_ez-Zor" title="Deir ez-Zor">Deir ez-Zor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deir_ez-Zor_Camps" title="Deir ez-Zor Camps">surrounding desert</a>. There is no evidence that the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> provided the extensive facilities and supplies that would have been necessary to sustain the life of hundreds of thousands of Armenian deportees during their forced march to the Syrian desert or after.<sup id="cite_ref-StarveNYT_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarveNYT-71">[68]</a></sup> By August 1915, <i>The New York Times</i> repeated an unattributed report that "the roads and the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> are strewn with corpses of exiles, and those who survive are doomed to certain death. It is a plan to exterminate the whole Armenian people".<sup id="cite_ref-PerishNYT_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PerishNYT-72">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Talaat_Pasha" title="Talaat Pasha">Talaat Pasha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Djemal_Pasha" title="Djemal Pasha">Djemal Pasha</a> were completely aware that by abandoning the Armenian deportees in the desert they were condemning them to certain death.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> A dispatch from a "high diplomatic source in Turkey, not American, reporting the testimony of trustworthy witnesses" about the plight of Armenian deportees in northern Arabia and the Lower Euphrates valley was extensively quoted by <i>The New York Times</i> in August 1916:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The witnesses have seen thousands of deported Armenians under tents in the open, in caravans on the march, descending the river in boats and in all phases of their miserable life. Only in a few places does the Government issue any rations, and those are quite insufficient. The people, therefore, themselves are forced to satisfy their hunger with food begged in that scanty land or found in the parched fields.</p>
<p>Naturally, the death rate from starvation and sickness is very high and is increased by the brutal treatment of the authorities, whose bearing toward the exiles as they are being driven back and forth over the desert is not unlike that of slave drivers. With few exceptions no shelter of any kind is provided and the people coming from a cold climate are left under the scorching desert sun without food and water. Temporary relief can only be obtained by the few able to pay officials.<sup id="cite_ref-StarveNYT_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarveNYT-71">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Similarly, Major General <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Freiherr_Kress_von_Kressenstein" title="Friedrich Freiherr Kress von Kressenstein">Friedrich Freiherr Kress von Kressenstein</a> noted that "The Turkish policy of causing starvation is an all too obvious proof, if proof was still needed as to who is responsible for the massacre, for the Turkish resolve to destroy the Armenians".<sup id="cite_ref-Dadrian_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dadrian-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:350</sup></p>
<p>German engineers and labourers involved in building the railway also witnessed Armenians being crammed into cattle cars and shipped along the railroad line. Franz Gunther, a representative for <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a> which was funding the construction of the Baghdad Railway, forwarded photographs to his directors and expressed his frustration at having to remain silent amid such "bestial cruelty".<sup id="cite_ref-Balakian_42-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakian-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:326</sup> Major General <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Lossow" title="Otto von Lossow">Otto von Lossow</a>, acting military attaché and head of the German Military <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">Plenipotentiary</a> in the Ottoman Empire, spoke to Ottoman intentions in a conference held in <a href="/wiki/Batum" class="mw-redirect" title="Batum">Batum</a> in 1918:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Turks have embarked upon the "total extermination of the Armenians in <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a>&#160;... The aim of Turkish policy is, as I have reiterated, the taking of possession of Armenian districts and the extermination of the Armenians. Talaat's government wants to destroy all Armenians, not just in Turkey but also outside Turkey. On the basis of all the reports and news coming to me here in <a href="/wiki/Tiflis" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiflis">Tiflis</a> there hardly can be any doubt that the Turks systematically are aiming at the extermination of the few hundred thousand Armenians whom they left alive until now.<sup id="cite_ref-Dadrian_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dadrian-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:349</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rape_during_the_Armenian_Genocide" title="Rape during the Armenian Genocide">Rape</a> was an integral part of the genocide;<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> military commanders told their men to "do to [the women] whatever you wish", resulting in widespread sexual abuse. Deportees were displayed naked in <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and sold as sex slaves in some areas, including <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> according to the report of the German consul there, constituting an important source of income for accompanying soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> Rössler, the German consul in Aleppo during the genocide, heard from an "objective" Armenian that around a quarter of young women, whose appearance was "more or less pleasing", were regularly raped by the gendarmes, and that "even more beautiful ones" were violated by 10–15 men. This resulted in girls and women being left behind dying.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentration_camps">Concentration camps</span></h4>
<p>A network of 25 concentration camps was set up by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> to dispose of the Armenians who had survived the deportations to their ultimate point.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> This network, situated in the region of Turkey's present-day borders with Iraq and Syria, was directed by <a href="/wiki/%C5%9E%C3%BCkr%C3%BC_Kaya" title="Şükrü Kaya">Şükrü Kaya</a>, one of Talaat Pasha's right-hand men. Some of the camps were only temporary transit points. Others, such as Radjo, Katma, and Azaz, were briefly used as mass graves and then vacated by autumn 1915. Camps such as Lale, Tefridje, Dipsi, Del-El, and <a href="/wiki/Ra%27s_al-%27Ayn_Camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Ra's al-'Ayn Camps">Ra's al-'Ayn</a> were built specifically for those whose life expectancy was just a few days.<sup id="cite_ref-Kotek_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kotek-78">[75]</a></sup> According to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hilmar_Kaiser&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hilmar Kaiser (page does not exist)">Hilmar Kaiser</a>, the Ottoman authorities refused to provide food and water to the victims, increasing the mortality rate, and Muslim men obtained Armenian women through recorded marriages, while the deaths of their husbands were not recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-BloxhamMoses2010_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloxhamMoses2010-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bernau, an American citizen of German descent, traveled to the areas where Armenians were incarcerated and wrote a report that was deemed factual by Rössler, the German Consul at Aleppo. He reports mass graves containing over 60,000 people in <a href="/wiki/Meskene" class="mw-redirect" title="Meskene">Meskene</a> and large numbers of mounds of corpses, as the Armenians died due to hunger and disease. He reported seeing 450 orphans, who received at most 150 grams of bread per day, in a tent of 5–6 square meters. <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">Dysentery</a> swept through the camp and days passed between the instances of distribution of bread to some. In "Abu Herrera", near Meskene, he described how the guards let 240 Armenians starve, and wrote that they searched "horse droppings" for grains.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confiscation_of_property">Confiscation of property</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Confiscated_Armenian_property_in_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Confiscated Armenian property in Turkey">Confiscated Armenian property in Turkey</a></div>
<p>The Tehcir Law brought some measures regarding the property of the deportees, but in September a new law was proposed. By means of the "Abandoned Properties" Law (Law Concerning Property, Dept's and Assets Left Behind Deported Persons, also referred as the "Temporary Law on Expropriation and Confiscation"), the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Government_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Imperial Government (Ottoman Empire)">Ottoman government</a> took possession of all "abandoned" Armenian goods and properties. Ottoman parliamentary representative <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Riza" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Riza">Ahmed Riza</a> protested this legislation:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is unlawful to designate the Armenian assets as "abandoned goods" for the Armenians, the proprietors, did not abandon their properties voluntarily; they were forcibly, compulsorily removed from their domiciles and exiled. Now the government through its efforts is selling their goods&#160;... If we are a constitutional regime functioning in accordance with constitutional law we can't do this. This is atrocious. Grab my arm, eject me from my village, then sell my goods and properties, such a thing can never be permissible. Neither the conscience of the Ottomans nor the law can allow it.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 13 September 1915, the Ottoman parliament passed the "Temporary Law of Expropriation and Confiscation", stating that all property, including land, livestock, and homes belonging to Armenians, was to be confiscated by the authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Dadrian_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dadrian-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:224</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_aid_to_victims">International aid to victims</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:They_Shall_Not_Perish.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/They_Shall_Not_Perish.png/220px-They_Shall_Not_Perish.png" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/They_Shall_Not_Perish.png/330px-They_Shall_Not_Perish.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/They_Shall_Not_Perish.png/440px-They_Shall_Not_Perish.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="833" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:They_Shall_Not_Perish.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fundraising poster for the <a href="/wiki/American_Committee_for_Relief_in_the_Near_East" class="mw-redirect" title="American Committee for Relief in the Near East">American Committee for Relief in the Near East</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/American_Committee_for_Armenian_and_Syrian_Relief" class="mw-redirect" title="American Committee for Armenian and Syrian Relief">American Committee for Armenian and Syrian Relief</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Committee_for_Armenian_and_Syrian_Relief" class="mw-redirect" title="American Committee for Armenian and Syrian Relief">American Committee for Armenian and Syrian Relief</a> (<i>ACASR</i>, also known as "Near East Relief"), established in 1915 just after the deportations began, was a charitable organization established to relieve the suffering of the peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-marrus_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marrus-82">[79]</a></sup> The organization was championed by Henry Morgenthau, Sr., American ambassador to the Ottoman Empire. Morgenthau's dispatches on the mass slaughter of Armenians galvanized much support for the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> In its first year, the ACRNE cared for 132,000 Armenian orphans from <a href="/wiki/Tiflis" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiflis">Tiflis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">Yerevan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sivas" title="Sivas">Sivas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>. A relief organization for refugees in the Middle East helped donate over $102 million (budget $117,000,000) [1930 value of dollar] to Armenians both during and after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Oren_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oren-84">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:336</sup> Between 1915 and 1930, ACRNE distributed humanitarian relief to locations across a wide geographical range, eventually spending over ten times its <a href="/wiki/File:Near_East_Relief_Report_1919_-_The_New_York_Times_Current_History_-_May_1919.png" title="File:Near East Relief Report 1919 - The New York Times Current History - May 1919.png">original estimate</a> and helping around 2,000,000 refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_.22Special_Organization.22">The "Special Organization"</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Special_Organization_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Special Organization (Ottoman Empire)">Special Organization (Ottoman Empire)</a></div>
